The Property Report
Most `Undervalued' Home Market Lacks Buyers --- Bryan-College Station, Texas Shows How Economic Tools Can Fail to Predict Prices
By James R. Hagerty
1210 words
14 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

COLLEGE STATION, Texas -- When economists in Cleveland and Boston recently ran the numbers, they concluded that houses in this college town were a huge bargain.

The analysis by National City Corp., a Cleveland banking concern, and Global Insight Inc., a Boston consulting firm, ranked the adjacent towns of Bryan and College Station -- home of Texas A&M University -- as the most "undervalued" housing market in the country in this year's first quarter. So, as the real-estate market cools along the East and West Coasts, are savvy investors racing here to buy every house in sight?

On a recent afternoon, there was little sign of a stampede.

Janet Higgins, a veteran real-estate broker here, drives a visitor past a four-bedroom home custom-built seven years ago, with marble floors and a "gourmet" kitchen. It is on sale for about $420,000, down from $440,000 last year. "It's a wonderful house," Mrs. Higgins sighs. "It's got all the whistles and bells." But she says it is tough competing with new homes on the edge of town. A few hundred feet away, she points to another mini-mansion: "This house they almost gave away."

Even by Texas standards, builders here respond quickly to any upturn in demand and give buyers plenty of choices. Mrs. Higgins and other local housing experts do think home prices in Bryan-College Station are likely to rise in the next few years. But they don't expect the kind of buying frenzies that engulfed California in the first half of this decade.

Over enchiladas at a new Mexican restaurant, Marty Cangelose, a home appraiser and chairman of the Bryan-College Station Chamber of Commerce, predicts the housing market will keep "trudging right along" with house-price increases of 3% to 4% a year.

Of course, forecasting home prices is as risky as predicting how many touchdowns this town's beloved Aggies will score this fall. A visit to this metropolitan area of about 190,000 people shows the dangers of relying too heavily on economic models to determine where house prices are headed in any given town.

The quarterly analysis of house prices by Global Insight and National City looks at the ratios of home prices to household income in 317 metro areas and seeks to explain the variations in those ratios on the basis of population density (an indication of how much land is available), income levels and past differences in prices that are caused by such things as climate and schools. Economists then estimate what home prices should be and compare those estimates with market prices to determine whether homes are overvalued or undervalued.

The latest study found Bryan-College Station was 24% undervalued. Dallas was the runner-up, at 19% undervalued. Indeed, eight of the 10 bargain-basement cities were in the Lone Star State. The most overvalued cities were Naples, Fla., and Salinas, Calif.

The study always gets headlines and fuels cocktail chatter. But Michael Carliner, an economist at the National Association of Home Builders, says the historical data crunched in this study may not have much to do with where prices are currently headed. The local price outlook, he says, depends on such hard-to-predict things as how the local economy will perform, population trends and the pace of home building.

Richard J. DeKaser, chief economist at National City, who devised the home-valuation model, says that even the best economic models are "gross oversimplifications of reality." Even so, he says the model provides a general sense of where houses are bargains and where they are overpriced. "I think this is about as good as [such models] get," Mr. DeKaser says.

One thing the model doesn't measure is that Bryan-College Station has lots of homes on the market. In April the number of homes available for sale was enough to last 6.8 months at the current sales rate, compared with a national average of six months and Texas's statewide average of five months. Elsewhere in Texas, inventories have declined over the past year, but they have held steady at six to seven months here.

David Lereah, chief economist at the National Association of Realtors, says about six months of inventory denotes a market roughly balanced between supply and demand. When inventories are above that level, buyers are more likely to drive a hard bargain.

Home builders here seem to have been optimistic about future demand. Mark Dotzour, chief economist at Texas A&M's real-estate center, says that in the first five years of the 1990s, one new home-building permit was issued for every 7.5 new people here. In the first half of this decade, the rate soared to one building permit for every 1.3 new people.

Larry Mariott, president of Mariott Homes Inc., which builds in this area, believes that there are more builders here per capita than in most other places and that they are willing to work for lower margins than construction firms accept elsewhere. Maybe it is because of the pleasant lifestyle here and optimism about growth prospects, he says.

Most of the local builders are tiny. But one of the nation's largest builders, D.R. Horton Inc., recently began churning out homes here, and that suggests supply will remain plentiful.

Economic models also can't precisely measure differences in land availability and regulation; Texas regulates land use less strictly than many other states.

Other factors may also help to explain why home prices have stayed low here. No interstate passes through the area, and the nearest major airport is about 90 miles away in Houston, reducing its appeal to high-flying businesspeople. And more than 50,000 university and junior-college students live here, accounting for more than a quarter of the population. Their generally modest needs ensure that a big share of the local housing stock is low-end.

There are some bullish signs for real-estate investors. Moody's Economy.com, a research firm in West Chester, Pa., projects that Bryan-College Station will rank among the top fifth of metropolitan areas in terms of employment growth in the five years ending in 2010. Texas A&M, one of the largest universities in the country, provides a steady source of employment and some high-tech spinoffs. And local Realtors believe that lots of wealthy A&M alumni eventually will retire here.

Investors from California have been buying houses here, especially since Bryan-College Station first hit the top of the "undervalued" chart early this year. Partly as a result, home prices "will be higher," says Chris Tesch, a RE/Max agent here. "I can about guarantee it."

Some Californians even snap up houses sight unseen. "We've sold properties through FedEx and the fax machine," says Eric Walley, a sales manager at Stylecraft Builders Inc.

But Mrs. Higgins, the veteran agent, urges outside investors not to get carried away. Those expecting mammoth price gains "aren't thinking it through," she says.

Moving the Market: Venture-Backed Start-Ups Shine
135 words
6 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The median valuation for venture-backed, start-up companies in the U.S. soared 20% to $18.4 million in the first quarter, the highest quarterly level since the fourth quarter of 2000.

Median valuations -- the amount investors think the companies are worth -- increased from $15.3 million in last year's first quarter, according to a study from VentureOne, which is owned by Dow Jones &Co., publisher of The Wall Street Journal. Company valuations averaged $23 million at the end of 2000 before dropping off during the dot-com bust. VentureOne measured company values before new investment.

The figures are another sign of increased venture-capital activity in Silicon Valley and other U.S. technology hot spots.

Economic Expansion Is Slowing, Home, Factory Readings Indicate
By Christopher Conkey
681 words
2 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Readings on home prices and manufacturing activity offered fresh evidence that the economy is cooling after a blazing start to the year.

Home prices rose 2.0% in the first quarter, the slowest such pace in two years, according to the Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight. As with other data suggesting a moderate slowdown in the housing market, the Ofheo report shows that the rate of appreciation has gradually declined since peaking last year. Still, the rate of growth in house-price appreciation has retreated only from extraordinary highs, and in the first quarter prices were 12.5% more than a year ago.

A second report showed that the manufacturing sector experienced slower growth. The Institute for Supply Management, a purchasing managers' trade group, said its index of manufacturing activity fell to 54.4 in May from 57.3 in April.

Lower readings on new orders, production and employment contributed to the decline, which left the index at its lowest point in nearly a year.

Together, the figures point to an economy shifting into a slower period of expansion after a robust first quarter.

"The rate of growth is slowing a little bit but we have to realize that we're coming from a first quarter when growth was very strong," said Norbert Ore, who oversees the ISM index and also serves as Georgia-Pacific Corp.'s procurement director. "At the end of the day, business spending is staying quite strong and the consumer is the wild card."

Because consumer purchases drive most economic growth, one of the major concerns among economists has been that the softening housing market and high gas prices will cause consumers to curtail their spending. Yesterday's Ofheo report alleviated some concerns on the housing side.

"These data show average housing prices still growing stronger than some might have expected," said James Lockhart, the office's acting director. If prices keep rising, consumers can continue tapping the equity built up in their houses to fuel other forms of spending.

Ofheo is considered the best authority on house prices because it best tracks the changing value of individual homes over time. Its index incorporates data from refinancings as well as purchase transactions. When refinancing data are removed, the index showed purchase prices up 10% in the first quarter compared with a year earlier.

Other measures have shown even less appreciation, and economists at UBS Securities LLC noted that the Ofheo index doesn't include condo and co-op prices, which have experienced some of the sharpest pullbacks.

Regardless of the measure used, however, a consistent theme of gradual cooling in the housing market emerges. "Increasing sales inventories are apparently giving buyers greater bargaining power, while increasing interest rates are dampening demand," said Patrick Lawler, chief economist at Ofheo.

In other signs of the housing slowdown, the Commerce Department said construction spending dropped 0.1% in April as residential home building fell 1.1%. Separately, the National Association of Realtors said its pending-home-sales index, a measure of future activity in the sector, was at 111.8 in April, down 3.7% from March and 11.7% from a year ago.

The ISM reading of 54.4 in May still points to growth for manufacturers, but several components provided reasons for concern. The new-orders index, a barometer of future production, fell to its lowest point in a year.

The employment index fell to 52.9 in May from 55.8 in April, raising more concerns about future growth. The index for prices, an indicator of potential inflationary pressures, jumped to 77.0 last month, its highest mark since the aftermath of Hurricane Katrina late last summer.

Other data released yesterday offered a more comforting signal on inflation. The Labor Department revised upward to 3.7% its measure of nonfarm business productivity in the first quarter, as output jumped 6.5% and hours worked rose 2.7%. Unit labor costs rose 1.6% during the first quarter.

Economy & Politics
U.S. reports indicate economy is cooling off --- Manufacturing data show slower growth; construction wanes
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
374 words
2 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
15
English
(c) 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

Economic data indicated a cooling U.S. economy, with slowing growth in the factory sector and a drop in construction spending underscoring the decelerating housing market. However, disparate data on inflationary pressures offered few clues for monetary-policy direction.

The Institute for Supply Management said its index of manufacturing activity fell to 54.4 in May from 57.3 in April and 55.2 in March. Readings above 50 in the ISM report indicate expanding activity.

"The manufacturing sector grew once again in May, but the rate of growth . . . slowed somewhat during the month," said Norbert Ore, who directs the survey for the ISM. "The slower growth is evidenced by a significant loss of momentum in the last four months" in the new-order index, while "prices, driven by raw-materials costs, are a concern," he said.

The group again reported that inflationary forces were pressuring factory operators. Its prices index hit 77 in May, up from 71.5 in April and the highest level since last fall.

Meanwhile, government data showed overall construction spending fell in April, marking the first monthly decline since June 2005 and bringing spending totals slightly below expectations. Spending fell 0.1% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.196 trillion, the Commerce Department said. Spending in March increased 0.9%, an unrevised figure.

Separately, U.S. productivity rose at a faster pace than previously estimated in the first quarter and labor costs eased, suggesting that greater efficiency of U.S. firms and workers is keeping a lid on inflationary pressures. The Labor Department said nonfarm business-sector productivity increased by an annualized 3.7% after falling 0.3% in the fourth quarter of 2005. The government had estimated first-quarter productivity growth at 3.2%.

Unit labor costs rose at an annual rate of 1.6% in the quarter, compared with a previous estimate of 2.5%.

The upward revision to productivity and downward revisions to labor costs could bolster the argument for Federal Reserve policy makers to pause in their nearly two-year-long tightening cycle.

Economy & Politics
U.S. factory sector cools, construction spending drops
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
640 words
2 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
13
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Economic data indicated a cooling U.S. economy, with slowing growth in the factory sector and an unexpected drop in construction spending underscoring the decelerating housing market. However, disparate data on inflationary pressures offered few clues for monetary-policy direction.

The Institute for Supply Management said its index of manufacturing activity fell to 54.4 in May from 57.3 in April and 55.2 in March. Readings above 50 in the ISM report indicate expanding activity.

"The manufacturing sector grew once again in May, but the rate of growth . . . slowed somewhat during the month," said Norbert Ore, who directs the survey for the ISM. "The slower growth is evidenced by a significant loss of momentum in the last four months" in the new-order index, while "prices, driven by raw-materials costs, are a concern," he said.

In the details of the report, the group again noted that inflationary forces were pressuring factory operators. The prices index hit 77 in May, up from 71.5 in April. May's reading was the highest since last fall.

The ISM also reported that its production index moved down to 57.2, compared with April's 60.4, while the new-orders index came in at 53.7, down from 57.6. The ISM's inventories index hit 48.0 in May. That index was 51.3 in April. The group's employment index stood at 52.9, representing slower hiring gains relative to April's 55.8.

Meanwhile, government data showed overall construction spending fell in April, marking the first monthly decline since June 2005 and bringing spending totals slightly below expectations. Spending fell 0.1% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.196 trillion, the Commerce Department said. Spending in March increased 0.9%, an unrevised figure. Wall Street had expected April construction spending would be flat.

April residential construction spending fell by 1.1% to $664.56 billion. Private residential construction, which accounts for the vast majority of residential building, had its largest decrease since January 2004. Residential spending rose 1.0% in March, revised lower than the previously reported 1.6% gain.

Separately, U.S. productivity rose at a faster pace than previously estimated last quarter and labor costs eased, suggesting that greater efficiency of U.S. firms and workers is keeping a lid on inflationary pressures.

The Labor Department said nonfarm business-sector productivity increased by an annualized 3.7% during the first quarter after falling 0.3% in the fourth quarter of 2005. The government had originally estimated first-quarter productivity growth at 3.2%. Wall Street economists had expected a revision to 3.9% due to an upward revision to first-quarter gross domestic product growth from 4.8% to 5.3%.

Unit labor costs rose at an annual rate of 1.6% in the first quarter, compared with a previous estimate of 2.5%. The figure for the fourth quarter of 2005 was revised sharply to an annualized decline of 0.6% compared with a previous estimate of a 3% rise.

The upward revision to productivity and downward revisions to labor costs could bolster the argument for Federal Reserve policy makers to pause in their nearly two-year-long tightening cycle when they meet at the end of the month.

In minutes of the May 10 Federal Open Market Committee meeting, which were released Wednesday, officials said "further productivity gains, which had remained fairly strong on balance in recent quarters may keep any acceleration in wages from seeping into higher prices. Still, officials expressed heightened concern over inflation trends, so some further signs of slower economic activity may be needed to keep officials from boosting official rates further despite the latest evidence of fast productivity growth.

Leading the News: U.S. growth may trip on housing stall
By Mark Whitehouse
434 words
26 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
2
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

It is official: The U.S. was one of the world's fastest-growing advanced economies in the first quarter of 2006. Downbeat data on home sales, however, support economists' expectations that the distinction won't last.

The U.S. Commerce Department Thursday revised to 5.3% its estimate of first-quarter growth in real gross domestic product -- a measure of the nation's output, adjusted for inflation -- from an initial estimate of 4.8%. The upward revision was smaller than expected, but still made the U.S. the second-fastest-growing economy among advanced members of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development that have reported first-quarter data. Greece is in first place, with an annualized growth rate of 8.2%, according to the OECD.

Economists see the big first-quarter number more as a one-time payback for an unusually slow fourth quarter -- which took the hit for Hurricane Katrina -- than an indication of how fast the U.S. economy will grow this year.

"It was a hot quarter, but it was a bit of a fluke and overstated the underlying strength of the U.S.," said Nariman Behravesh, chief economist at consultancy Global Insight. Combined, the two quarters yielded average annualized growth of 3.5%, about the rate at which the U.S. economy has tended to expand in the long term.

The first-quarter spurt contributed to another strong quarter for U.S. business. After-tax corporate profits rose 8.8% in the first quarter to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.115 trillion. That was less than the 13.8% rise in the fourth quarter. But profits in the latest period were up 24.8% from a year earlier, the largest year-on-year increase since 2002. At 8.9%, after-tax corporate profits' share of the entire economy reached the highest level in at least 60 years.

New data on home sales, however, supported economists' predictions that the economy is likely cooling. The National Association of Realtors reported Thursday that sales of existing, or previously owned, homes fell 2% in April to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 6.76 million units.

Housing has been a major pillar of the economy in recent years as low mortgage rates fueled a home-buying boom that created lots of new jobs in everything from construction to mortgage finance. As home values rose, consumers were able to tap the equity in their homes to finance spending. A cooling housing market is seen damping consumer spending.

GDP Growth Rate Revised Upward, but Housing Slowdown Looms
By Mark Whitehouse
747 words
26 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

It is official: The U.S. was one of the world's fastest-growing advanced economies in the first quarter of 2006. Downbeat data on home sales, however, support economists' expectations that the distinction won't last.

The Commerce Department yesterday revised its estimate to an annualized rate of 5.3% of first-quarter growth in real gross domestic product -- a measure of the nation's output, adjusted for inflation -- from an initial estimate of 4.8%. The upward revision was smaller than expected, but still made the U.S. the second-fastest-growing economy among advanced members of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development that have reported first-quarter data. Greece is in first place, with an annualized growth rate of 8.2%, according to the OECD.

Economists see the first-quarter number more as a one-time payback for an unusually slow fourth quarter -- which took the hit for Hurricane Katrina -- than an indication of how fast the economy will grow this year.

"It was a hot quarter, but it was a bit of a fluke and overstated the underlying strength of the U.S.," said Nariman Behravesh, chief economist at consultancy Global Insight. Combined, the two quarters yielded average annualized growth of 3.5%, about the rate at which the economy has tended to expand in the long term.

The first-quarter spurt contributed to another strong quarter for business. After-tax corporate profits rose 8.8% in the first quarter to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.115 trillion. That was less than the 13.8% rise in the fourth quarter. But profits in the latest period were up 24.8% from a year earlier, the largest year-on-year increase since 2002. At 8.9%, after-tax corporate profits' share of the entire economy reached the highest level in at least 60 years.

New data on home sales, however, supported economists' predictions that the economy is likely cooling. The National Association of Realtors reported yesterday that sales of existing, or previously owned, homes fell 2% in April to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 6.76 million units.

Housing has been a major pillar of the economy in recent years as low mortgage rates fueled a home-buying boom that created lots of new jobs in everything from construction to mortgage finance. As home values rose, consumers were able to tap the equity in their homes to finance spending. A cooling housing market is expected to damp consumers' desire to spend.

Most home prices still are rising, but a growing backlog of unsold properties is putting more pressure on prices to fall. The median single-family home price in April was $222,700, up 4.3% from a year earlier, while the median price of condominiums and cooperatives was $222,000, down 0.2% from a year ago. At April's rate of sales, it would take six months to sell all the homes on the market, up from 5.6 months in March. The inventory of single-family homes, at 5.9 months, was up 40.5% from a year earlier -- the largest year-on-year rise since 1982, when the Realtors' association began collecting the data.

"What's interesting is the unbelievable speed with which people are dumping houses on the market," said Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at consultancy High Frequency Economics. "I don't think the market can absorb this much supply this quickly."

The factors that led to the bump up in GDP also point to slower growth ahead. A smaller-than-expected trade deficit reflected some strength in U.S. exports, but was driven mostly by a drop in oil imports that economists expect will reverse in coming months. Businesses produced more to rebuild inventories depleted by robust first-quarter sales, but that means they won't need to restock as much in the current quarter or later.

"The upward revisions leave a little less growth on the table for the remainder of 2006," said Steve Wieting, senior economist at Citigroup in New York.

Separately, a report on jobless claims suggested companies might be slowing their hiring. The Labor Department reported that 329,000 people filed for unemployment benefits in the week ended May 20. The seasonally adjusted number was down 40,000 from the previous week, but higher than economists had expected.

Economy & Politics
U.S. data cause surprise --- Though figures defy expectations, trend is still for slowdown
By Mark Whitehouse
438 words
25 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal Europe

8
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The latest readings on U.S. housing and business investment confounded economists' expectations, but the underlying trends still show the economy acting pretty much as Federal Reserve officials had hoped: Perhaps slowing, but not sinking.

In two separate reports, the Commerce Department said new-home sales rose and businesses' orders for capital goods dropped in April. At first glance, that's at odds with the Fed's main scenario for what the economy will do this year: Consumer spending should slow as a weakening housing market makes people feel poorer, but increased business investment -- in the form of capital-goods purchases and hiring -- should help keep the deceleration from becoming a disaster.

Economists, however, warned against reading too much into the volatile monthly data, saying that longer-term trends still point toward slowing home sales and growing capital spending. "We all agree that the housing market is cooling off, and some other sector of the economy must take the burden of keeping growth healthy," said Ethan Harris, chief U.S. economist at Lehman Brothers in New York. "Capital spending is a prime candidate."

New-home sales rose to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.19 million in April, up 4.9% from the previous month, thanks in part to a surge in southern states. The number contrasted sharply with expectations of an April drop in home sales, but downward revisions to previous months' numbers lessened the surprise.

Meanwhile, a sharp drop in aircraft business helped U.S. total orders for durable goods, defined as anything intended to last three years or more, fall a larger-than-expected 4.8% to a seasonally adjusted $210 billion in April, after rising an upwardly revised 6.6% in March. One measure of business investment -- "core" orders for capital goods such as computers, oil rigs and railroad cars, excluding defense and aircraft -- also dropped a larger-than-expected 1.7% in April, compared to a revised 3.6% increase the previous month.

Economists saw April's drop in capital-goods orders largely as payback for the large jump in March. Overall, capital spending remains on an upward trend: In this year's first four months, core capital-goods orders were up 9.7% on year.

"If we smooth out the vagaries of month-to-month flows, what emerges is a pattern that is wholly consistent with the Fed's belief of how the economy is going to unfold this year," said David Resler, chief economist at Nomura Securities in New York.

The Economy
New-Home Sales Rose in April As Capital-Goods Orders Fell --- Data Defy Expectations, But Longer-Term Trends Line Up With Fed Outlook
By Mark Whitehouse
719 words
25 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The latest readings on U.S. housing and business investment confounded economists' expectations, but the underlying trends still show the economy acting pretty much as Federal Reserve officials had hoped: Perhaps slowing, but not sinking.

In two separate reports, the Commerce Department said new-home sales rose and businesses' orders for capital goods dropped in April. At first glance, that's at odds with the Fed's main scenario for what the economy will do this year: Consumer spending should slow as a weakening housing market makes people feel poorer, but increased business investment -- in the form of capital-goods purchases and hiring -- should help keep the deceleration from becoming a disaster.

Economists, however, warned against reading too much into the volatile monthly data, saying that longer-term trends still point toward slowing home sales and growing capital spending. "We all agree that the housing market is cooling off, and some other sector of the economy must take the burden of keeping growth healthy," said Ethan Harris, chief U.S. economist at Lehman Brothers in New York. "Capital spending is a prime candidate."

New-home sales rose to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.19 million in April, up 4.9% from the previous month, thanks in part to a surge in Southern states. The number contrasted sharply with expectations of an April drop in home sales, but downward revisions to the previous months' numbers lessened the surprise. After revisions, the rate of new-home sales dropped 7.6% over the first three months of 2006, compared with a previous estimate of 4.2%. April's rate of sales was still 12.4% below the most recent peak in July 2005.

"The overall picture is one of a cooling housing market," said Joshua Shapiro, chief U.S. economist at MFR Inc. a New York consultancy. "It's hard to argue against that." Declining demand, for example, was among the reasons cited by luxury-home builder Toll Brothers Inc. when it announced earlier this week that it was lowering its full-year earnings forecast.

Meanwhile, a sharp drop in aircraft business helped U.S. total orders for durable goods, defined as anything intended to last three years or more, fall a larger-than-expected 4.8% to a seasonally adjusted $210 billion in April, after rising an upwardly revised 6.6% in March. One measure of business investment -- "core" orders for capital goods such as computers, oil rigs and railroad cars, excluding defense and aircraft -- also dropped a larger-than-expected 1.7% in April, compared with a revised 3.6% increase the previous month.

Economists saw April's drop in capital-goods orders largely as payback for the large jump in March. Overall, capital spending remains on an upward trend: In this year's first four months, core capital-goods orders were up 9.7% over the same period last year.

"If we smooth out the vagaries of month-to-month flows, what emerges is a pattern that is wholly consistent with the Fed's belief of how the economy is going to unfold this year," said David Resler, chief economist at Nomura Securities in New York.

Increasing demand for U.S. capital goods might illustrate a second-order effect of globalization, say some economists. The rise of export-oriented producers in China and elsewhere created a flood of cheap consumer goods into the U.S., increasing competition for the U.S. businesses and holding prices down. Now, though, as those same foreign producers invest to boost capacity, they're also boosting demand -- and prices -- for the kind of capital goods that the U.S. makes.

"We're seeing very strong demand for capital goods in Asian markets," both from Western firms building local facilities and from local firms looking to compete with the foreigners, said Edward Campbell, chief executive officer of Nordson Corp., Westlake, Ohio, which makes equipment that applies paint and other coatings to everything from cereal boxes to washing machines. He said his company's Asian orders were up 48% in the most recent quarter from a year earlier.

Economy & Politics
U.S. economy shows life --- Data have surprises, but trend remains set on a slowdown
By Mark Whitehouse
445 words

The Wall Street Journal Asia

The Property Report: High-end-home sales in the U.S. buck cooling trend in the market
By Troy McMullen
348 words
24 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
29
English
(c) 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

Despite growing indications of a cooling housing market in the U.S., one niche continues to sell briskly -- multimillion-dollar homes.

In San Francisco, 18 homes costing $3 million or more sold in the first quarter, up from 15 in the same period last year, according to real-estate-information service DataQuick. In Jackson, Wyoming, that number rose to 21 homes from 17, according to Jackson Hole Real Estate & Appraisal. Higher up the scale, 10 homes at $5 million or more in Palm Beach, Florida, sold in the first quarter, up from eight last year, says the county assessor's office.

One factor in the growth could be that median prices of all homes have risen, pushing more homes into the luxury end. Also, inventory is up across the board. And it is true that not all markets are seeing a surge in high-end sales. In New York's borough of Manhattan, 212 homes priced above $4 million sold in the first quarter, from 226 in the year-earlier period, according to Brown Harris Stevens.

But at a time when the overall number of home sales has declined in many U.S. cities, the number in the ultrahigh range has continued to grow.

Gregory Heym, chief economist for New York real-estate brokerages Brown Harris Stevens and Halstead Property, says there may be less of a connection between the highest segment and the rest of the housing market. The stock market has created new wealth and the number of millionaires has grown, so more buyers are paying in cash. That has left luxury buyers mostly insulated from economic factors such as rising short-term interest rates.

Mark Zandi, chief economist at forecasting firm Moody's Economy.com, says the segment of high-end buyers "won't be immune from the unfolding travails of the rest of the market, but it will weather those difficulties much better than it has historically."

The Home Front:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
The Home Front: A Different Path for High-End Homes
By Troy McMullen
1585 words
19 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
W10
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

DESPITE GROWING indications of a cooling housing market, one niche continues to sell briskly -- multimillion-dollar homes.

Over the past few months in the overall U.S. real-estate market, more homes have crowded the market and sales volumes have fallen in areas from Houston to Boston and Washington, D.C. Freddie Mac, the government-sponsored provider of mortgage-loan funding, predicts total home sales this year will be down by about 7% from 2005's record levels. Yet one area of the market appears immune to all that: In many locations, homes on the ultrahigh end of the price scale -- those costing $3 million and up -- have been selling in increasing numbers.

In San Francisco, 18 homes in that range sold in the first quarter, up from 15 in the same period last year, according to real-estate information service DataQuick. In Jackson, Wyo., that number rose to 21 homes from 17, according to Jackson Hole Real Estate and Appraisal. Higher up the scale, 10 homes at $5 million or more in Palm Beach, Fla., sold in the first quarter, up from eight last year, says the county assessor's office.

One factor in the growth could be that median prices of all homes have risen, pushing more homes into the luxury end. Also, inventory is up across the board. But at a time when the overall number of home sales has declined in many markets, the number in the ultra-high range has continued to grow. One possible message: Just as it is often said that the rich aren't like the rest of us, the real-estate market of the rich appears to bear a decreasing resemblance to the one experienced by most Americans.

Homes at $3 million and up represent less than 2% of the overall market, estimates the National Association of Realtors. Activity at this small upper end has traditionally been a leading indicator for the broader market, says Gregory Heym, chief economist for the New York real-estate brokerages Brown Harris Stevens and Halstead Property. Now, he says, there may be less of a connection between the two segments. The stock market has created new wealth and the number of millionaires has grown, so more buyers are paying in cash. (The National Association of Realtors found that 8% of home buyers paid in cash last year, up from 6% in 2003.) That has left luxury buyers mostly insulated from economic factors such as rising short-term interest rates.

Mark Zandi, chief economist at forecasting firm Moody's Economy.com, says the segment of high-end buyers "won't be immune from the unfolding travails of the rest of the market, but it will weather those difficulties much better than it has historically."

Walter Molony, a spokesman for the National Association of Realtors, says the highly volatile high-end market serves as a poor market indicator. Activity among first-time home-buyers, he says, is more telling. "That segment provides liquidity for people to be able to trade up to larger homes," he says. "Without strong entry-level activity, the market would sink."

When Todd Michael Glaser listed his 11-bedroom Miami home in February, overall sales volume in the city was slipping -- sales fell 21% for the month over a year earlier. But he wasn't concerned. He listed it for $40 million, well above Miami-Dade County's record single-family home sale of $27.5 million in 1999.

Mr. Glaser, a 41-year-old real-estate investor, figured the property would sell based on its amenities and location. The 20,000-square-foot home is one of the biggest on North Bay Road, where neighbors include Billy Joel and Matt Damon. A month after hitting the market, it went under contract for purchase. Brokers with knowledge of the deal put the price at over $30 million, though Mr. Glaser wouldn't reveal the number. "It's not a property for the everyday home buyer," he says.

In Los Angeles County, 217 homes priced over $3 million sold during the first quarter, up from 114 during the same period last year, according to Cecelia Kennelly-Waeschle of Sotheby's International Realty. That is the biggest first-quarter jump since the firm started tracking sales in 1988. For the same period, the number of all sales in the county fell 10.3%, according to DataQuick. Two homes priced above $10 million sold in Santa Barbara, Calif., during the first quarter -- including a $28.5 million, 17-acre oceanfront property to actor Kevin Costner -- up from one a year earlier. (The data in this and other markets do not show how long the homes spent on the market or whether they sold at their original asking price.)

Some affluent buyers don't limit themselves to what's on the market. When Henry Kravis, managing partner of New York-based Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, went shopping for a Palm Beach house in January, he didn't like any of the available properties. His broker, Lawrence Moens, identified a property that wasn't for sale, but fit Mr. Kravis's criteria: a 15,255-square-foot home on five acres along Lake Worth. "I just knocked on the door and said, `I've got a buyer willing to pay a lot of money for your home,' " says Mr. Moens. A few weeks later, the deal closed for $50 million, public records show. Local brokers say it is the highest price ever paid for a non-oceanfront property there. Mr. Kravis declined to comment.

Not all markets are seeing a surge in high-end sales. In Manhattan, 212 homes priced above $4 million sold in the first quarter, from 226 in the year-earlier period, according to Brown Harris Stevens. Appraisers say that apartments are staying on the market longer.

Even where sales are falling, confidence hasn't always flagged. On the Nevada side of Lake Tahoe, sales of homes priced above $1 million fell 35% in the first quarter over a year earlier, according to Chase International Realty. That didn't stop Tom Gonzales from raising the price on his home in Incline Village, Nev. After staying on the market for a year at $60 million, he raised the price on the 4.5-acre property to $65 million last month, to account for the upkeep he's paid. "I don't think there's a shortage of people looking for a property like this," says Mr. Gonzales, 61, who co-founded software company Commerce One in the 1990s.

Yet in Fairfield County, Conn., Lake Forest, Ill., and San Diego County, brokers say many sellers are trimming prices amid a glut of pricey homes. Writer Jane Green and her husband, bank executive David Burke, cut the price of their Westport, Conn., property by $1 million after it sat for eight months at $5 million. Shortly afterwards, the property sold in January for $3.9 million, public records show.

Talk of a slowdown hasn't affected Sean Wolfington, a former Philadelphia Internet entrepreneur, who just outbid two other buyers on a six-bedroom estate in Key Biscayne, Fla., formerly owned by the singer Cher. The cost: $8.8 million, in cash. "Interest rates aren't a factor for me," says Mr. Wolfington, 34, who now runs an independent film company. "Waterfront properties like these are in limited supply. I saw this as an excellent buying opportunity."

Economy & Politics
U.S. producers absorb some pain of energy prices
By Mark Whitehouse
521 words
17 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
11
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. producers absorbed some of the pain of higher energy prices in April and the housing market showed further signs of cooling, helping to quell fears inflation will spread to the broader U.S. economy.

Wholesale prices of finished energy goods such as gasoline rose 4% in April, pushing up average prices on all finished goods -- everything from turkeys to tractors -- a greater-than-expected 0.9% in April, the Labor Department reported. But the closely watched "core" index of producer prices, which excludes energy and food, rose only 0.1%, below expectations.

"Given all the energy-price hikes coming through, that's still very subdued," said Nigel Gault, U.S. economist at consultancy Global Insight.

Economists and investors watch wholesale prices closely as a signal of what may happen with the consumer-price index, a broader measure of inflation on which the government will report today. The two don't correlate closely from month to month, but producer prices do give some indication of what's coming in consumer prices.

In recent weeks, various U.S. indicators have pointed toward gathering inflation pressures, raising concerns that the Federal Reserve will have to extend its 23-month string of interest-rate increases. Also, the U.S. economy appears to have been running a lot hotter in the first quarter than initially believed. The Bureau of Economic Analysis reported last month that real gross domestic product -- the value of the nation's output, adjusted for inflation -- grew at an annual rate of 4.8% in the January-March period. Some expect the government to revise its estimate of first-quarter GDP growth a full percentage point higher.

Longer term, many economists are counting on a cooling housing market to ease the economy into a more moderate, less inflationary rate of growth. The Commerce Department yesterday provided some data supporting that scenario: Housing starts fell to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.85 million in April, below expectations and down 7.4% from March.

"That's moving in the right direction," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Economy.com. "Unless housing comes off quickly, the broader economy will remain strong, and inflationary pressures and interest rates will continue to rise."

Yesterday's reports on wholesale prices and housing had a calming effect on U.S. markets. In early futures trading, the implied probability that the Fed will raise its short-term interest-rate target to 5.25% from the current 5% at its next policy meeting, in June, fell to 36% from 42%. The yield on the 10-year Treasury note fell to 5.13% from 5.16%.

U.S. producers kept prices of finished goods largely in check despite larger increases in input prices, suggesting they are finding ways to absorb higher costs. The average price of core crude materials -- the most basic inputs such as wheat and timber -- rose 4.7% in April, while the average price of core intermediate materials such as processed yarns and ball bearings were up 0.4%.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

World Currency Trading: Dollar drops amid weakness in U.S. housing
By Wanfeng Zhou
229 words
17 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
22
English
(c) 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

NEW YORK -- The dollar fell against major rivals after U.S. government reports for April showed core wholesale inflation remained tame while housing starts dropped for a third-straight month.

At midmorning, the dollar was at 110.08 yen, off from 110.35 yen late Monday in New York. The euro was at $1.2851, up from $1.2784. The pound was at $1.8853, up from $1.8773. The dollar was at 1.2076 Swiss francs, down from 1.2124 francs.

In the U.S., "the sharper-than-expected weakness in housing signals fresh weakness is ahead for the dollar," said Brian Dolan, head of currency research at Gain Capital. The data suggest "the slowing in the housing market that the [Federal Reserve] has anticipated is materializing," he added.

Housing starts fell 7.4% in April to a seasonally adjusted 1.85 million annualized units, the U.S. Commerce Department reported. Led by higher energy costs, producer prices rose 0.9% in April, but prices outside of energy were well contained, the Labor Department said.

The euro felt some pressure after a poll of German sentiment fell sharply. The report raised concern that the European economic recovery isn't as strong as some in the market had expected.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Justin Lahart
422 words
16 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

[Today's Market Forecast]

Desperate House-builders

Usually, strong housing reports are good news for the economy. Sometimes, a little weakness is in order.

Today's report on housing starts from the Commerce Department might be a case in point. Under normal circumstances, a strong number would be cheered as a sign that the housing market is holding up. But aggressive construction now would hint at recklessness among the nation's home builders.

Economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires and CNBC estimate that construction began on 1.95 million homes last month, at an annual rate, not far off the March pace of 1.96 million. The government's measure of housing starts surged in January -- thanks to mild weather and to statistical adjustments used to factor in seasonal building patterns -- and then fell sharply in February and March, as cold weather rolled in and the housing market continued to slow.

Housing looks as if it's getting slower still. On Friday, the University of Michigan's twice-monthly survey of consumer sentiment showed that, as of mid-May, Americans believed this was the worst time to buy a home since 1990, which was the beginning of the last major housing slowdown. Yesterday, the National Association of Home Builders' Housing Market Index, which is based on home builders' assessment of the market, slipped to its lowest level since 1995. And in recent months, several large public home builders have said their orders for new homes are slipping.

With the market cooling and the home builders themselves feeling pessimistic, a dip in housing starts is probably in order. It would suggest that builders are drawing down excess inventory. Home builders haven't always acted so rationally in the past. In the early 1990s, many small home builders put up houses before they found actual buyers, and were rewarded by going out of business.

The hope is that home builders will be on better behavior this time. Large public builders now drive the market, and they say they're careful about managing inventories. Moreover, a dearth of available land and regulatory constraints may have damped speculative building during the boom.

Today's numbers will give a clue about whether that's true. But, as MFR economist Josh Shapiro says, you won't really know "until demand evaporates and you see who's naked and who's not."

---

Personal Finance
Growth in U.S. Home Prices Slows --- Some Markets Start to Cool As Inventory Levels Rise; Low-Cost Cities See Uptick
By Michael Corkery
607 words
16 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Home prices were higher in many U.S. markets in the first quarter of 2006, but the pace of growth is cooling.

In its latest report of home prices in 149 metropolitan areas, the National Association of Realtors said the median price of a single-family home in the U.S. was $217,900 in the first quarter, up 10.3% when compared with the same quarter a year ago. That's a smaller increase than in the fourth quarter, when the median price of a home was up about 13.6% year-over-year.

David Lereah, NAR's chief economist, said in a statement that home prices are rising less rapidly because the inventory of homes available for sale is rising. "With the supply of homes picking up very nicely in many areas of the country, pressure is coming off of home prices." Mr. Lereah also indicated that the trend is continuing into the current quarter, and that he expects price-appreciation will be "returning to normal rates of price growth in the single-digit range."

Several Florida metropolitan areas remained near the top of the list of cities with the strongest price appreciation in the first quarter, including Orlando, Tampa and Daytona Beach. And while price gains still posted double-digits in those cities, they were not as sizzling as in the fourth quarter. Price appreciation in the Cape Coral/Fort Myers areas, for example, was up 18.3% year-over-year in the first quarter compared with 48% in the fourth-quarter. In the Miami-Fort Lauderdale market, prices increased 11% year-over-year in the first quarter, compared with 24% in the fourth quarter.

The Phoenix area had the highest home-price appreciation in the first quarter, rising 38.4% to $268,300, compared with a year earlier. But that was down from a jump of 49% in the fourth quarter.

The weakest housing markets tended to be in the Midwest, especially cities in Ohio, Michigan and Indiana. But some coastal cities also had price declines, including the Boston area where the median price was $390,400, down 1.5% compared with a year earlier. While that price drop was relatively small, "if it continues for the next few quarters, it would be a worrisome sign that demand is just not matching up with the supply," said Lawrence Yun, another NAR economist.

Economists attributed price drops in parts of the Midwest to a sluggish economy. Richard DeKaser, the chief economist at Cleveland-based National City Corp., said the price slowdown in Boston "simply could be an affordability question -- prices outstripping people's ability to afford a home."

Affordability also could help to explain why prices are starting to pick up in some smaller low-cost cities that missed much of the housing boom of recent years. Prices in Elmira, N.Y. -- in western upstate New York -- were up 18.8% to a median of $88,500. The NAR said that was the largest price increase of any city in the Northeast.

Meanwhile, U.S. home builders turned negative on the housing market for the first time since just after the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks, the National Association of Home Builders and Wells Fargo said yesterday. The NAHB/Wells Fargo housing market index, which gauges builders' sentiment, fell six points in May from a revised 51 to 45, the lowest level since June 1995, according to the industry group.

Personal Finance
Housing Market Shows More Signs Of Slowing Down
By Michael Corkery
378 words
3 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. housing market is showing more signs of a slowdown.

The National Association of Realtors said yesterday that its index of pending sales of existing homes fell 6% in March compared with the same month last year. Meantime, another large home builder reported an expected drop in contracts for new homes.

The index of pending home sales is based on contracts for existing homes that were signed in March. The index declined 1.2% from February. "This squares with our overall perception of the ongoing, but moderate, settling of the market," said Dave Seiders, chief economist at the National Association of Home Builders.

David Lereah, chief economist at the National Association of Realtors, said in a statement that "the pending-sales data is showing a dampening effect from rising mortgage interest rates."

Last week the Commerce Department said new-home sales in March were up nearly 14% from February, but inventories of new homes on the market rose to 555,000 in March, up 24% from a year ago.

Ivy Zelman, an analyst at Credit Suisse, said some large home builders could be experiencing a more acute slowdown in sales because much of their business is concentrated in the once-hottest markets that are now cooling the fastest, such as California, Florida, Washington, D.C., and Arizona.

The latest home-sales report comes after Hovnanian Enterprises Inc., based in Red Bank, N.J., said Monday it expects a 20% decline in net contracts of new homes in its second quarter, partly because of increases in cancellation rates. The company lowered its fiscal 2006 guidance to $7.20 to $7.40 a share, from its earlier estimate of $8.05 to $8.40 a share.

On Friday, Toll Brothers Inc., the Horsham, Pa., luxury home builder, is expected to report its revenue, backlog and contracts for its second quarter. Analysts will be watching the report closely. "Each data point we get we are using to try to read the tea leaves," says Ms. Zelman.

Economy & Politics
Builders give U.S. economy a lift --- Record construction, manufacturing gain point to more growth
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
389 words
2 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
11
English
(c) 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

The U.S. continued showing signs of economic growth in April and March, as manufacturing picked up steam, beating expectations, while builders boosted construction spending to a record and consumers opened their wallets even wider.

The Institute for Supply Management, a private research group, said its index of manufacturing activity hit 57.3 in April, rising from 55.2 in March and 56.7 in February. Readings above 50 point to expansion in activity and describe the breadth of change.

Economists expected the index to come in at 54.8.

"The manufacturing sector grew at a faster rate during April, as production and employment showed significant strength," said Ralph Kauffman, who directs the ISM manufacturing survey. "While many members indicate that business is good, they still have major concerns about the impact of higher prices for energy and industrial commodities."

Meanwhile, total construction spending rose 0.9% in March to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.199 trillion, the U.S. Commerce Department said. February spending was revised to 1% from the previously reported 0.8% rise. Wall Street had expected that construction spending increased 0.4% in March.

Residential-construction spending advanced 1.6% in March to $680 billion, after a 1.3% rise in February. Nonresidential-construction spending was flat in March. Outlays for lodging, public safety and communication rose, but spending on roads declined.

U.S. personal income also increased in March, at a rate double what was expected. Spending also rose. Personal income increased at a seasonally adjusted rate of 0.8%, after rising an unrevised 0.3% rise in February, the Commerce Department said. March personal consumption rose 0.6%, after a revised 0.2% increase the month before.

The median forecast of 14 economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires and CNBC was for a 0.4% rise in personal income and in spending.

Savings as a percentage of aftertax income rose to negative 0.3% in March, compared with negative 0.6% in February.

Global chip sales rise 7.3%, driven by cellphones

2 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE

English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

World-wide chip sales rose 7.3% to $59.1 billion in the first quarter from a year ago, fueled by demand for cellphone chips, according to the Semiconductor Industry Association.

March sales of semiconductors also rose 7.3%, increasing from a year earlier to $19.7 billion, and they moved up 2.3% from February sales of $19.26 billion.

The San Jose, California, chip industry's trade group said cellphone sales in the first quarter increased 31% from the year-earlier quarter, helped by a substantially shorter replacement cycle and robust demand in China. Unit sales are now expected to reach one billion this year, with cellphones representing the second-largest market, after personal computers, for semiconductors.

March chip sales in the Asian-Pacific region climbed 13% to $8.94 billion from the year-earlier month and rose 1.7% from February. Japanese semiconductor sales fell 1.1% to $3.79 billion in March from a year earlier and rose 4% from February. March chip sales in the Americas rose 15% to $3.73 billion from a year ago and 0.4% from February.

Sales in Europe fell 4% to $3.24 billion in March from a year ago and rose 4.1% from February.

Total first-quarter semiconductor sales fell 1.3% from the fourth quarter of 2005, which the trade group said reflected normal seasonal patterns.

The Economy
Economy's Hot Streak Continues --- Business Spending May Take The Lead From Consumers; Inflation Specter Still Lurks
By Mark Whitehouse
776 words
2 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The first significant piece of economic data for April reinforced the widening view that the U.S. economy entered the second quarter with a good deal of momentum. But a whiff of inflation fueled concern that the Federal Reserve will be forced to step harder on the brakes.

The Institute for Supply Management, a purchasing managers' trade group, said yesterday that its index of manufacturing activity came in at 57.3 for April, higher than expected and up from 55.2 in March. Any level above 50 suggests that the manufacturing sector is growing. A reading in the mid-50s is strong for an economy already in its fifth year of expansion.

"It's an impressive number," said Peter Kretzmer, senior economist at Bank of America in New York. "With businesses feeling good and starting to hire, that adds to personal income and is likely to keep consumer spending going, even if other elements are slowing it down."

A drop in the new-orders component of the ISM index, however, suggests that April's number might be more an echo of a strong first quarter than a sign of things to come. "We'll have to see how well that translates into future growth," said Norbert Ore, who oversees the index and serves as procurement director at Georgia-Pacific Corp.

Separately, two reports from the Commerce Department showed U.S. consumers going strong in March. Personal consumption rose 0.6%, and a jump in home improvements helped boost construction spending by 0.9%. Both numbers were higher than economists' expectations for the month.

Economists said that after adjusting for inflation, personal spending was on a trajectory to rise at an annualized rate of about 3% in the second quarter, compared with 5.5% in the first quarter. That is consistent with expectations that the economy's rate of growth will slow in the current quarter, as higher gasoline prices and a cooling housing market make consumers feel poorer.

Personal income rose 0.8% in March, double the rate economists expected. A $40 billion annualized jump in payments under Medicare prescription-drug benefits accounted for most of the increase, but wages and salaries also rose at a healthy 0.4% rate, indicating that consumers are benefiting from a stronger job market. People kept spending beyond their immediate means: Expenditures exceeded disposable income by $32.5 billion, or 0.3%.

The Commerce Department also reported a larger-than-expected increase in its index of consumer-goods prices -- a number that Fed officials watch closely for signs of inflationary pressures. The "core" index, which excludes food and fuel, rose 0.3% in March and 2% for the 12 months ending in March, at the top of the 1% to 2% range that the Fed considers acceptable.

The inflation number led more investors to bet that the Fed will take its short-term interest-rate target a notch higher, to 5.25%, at its June policy-making meeting, after an expected increase to 5% from 4.75% at its May 10 meeting. In early afternoon trading, the futures market put the probability of a June increase at about one-in-three, compared with odds of about one-in-four last week.

In statements to Congress last week, Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke suggested that the Fed might take a break in June from two years of consistent rate increases, but the new inflation data will "make the case for a pause at the June meeting a little difficult," said John Ryding, chief U.S. economist at Bear Stearns in New York.

Until recently, U.S. manufacturers' good fortunes hadn't translated much into jobs and gains in household income, as companies found ways to produce more with the same workers and equipment. But recent robust jobs data suggest that they are getting to the point where they need to boost capacity to keep growing -- a factor that could mitigate any slowdown in consumer spending. In March, companies added 211,000 jobs to nonfarm payrolls. The government will release April jobs data Friday.

"We try to do lean manufacturing, but there's a point where you're bumping up against capacity and you're going to need to add more people," said Tony Raimondo Jr., president of Behlen Country, a division of Behlen Mfg. Co., of Columbus, Neb. He said Behlen Country, which makes ranch equipment such as horse stalls and dog kennels, is already hiring at one plant in Oregon.

Economy's Surge Stirs Questions About When Slowdown May Come
By Christopher Conkey and Greg Ip
1261 words
27 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The latest readings suggest the nation's economy is carrying significant momentum into the second quarter, raising new questions about when a slowdown might come after a torrid start to the year.

By most accounts, the economy surged in the first quarter after a brief lull late last year. When the government releases its first estimate tomorrow, economists expect it to say the economy grew at a roughly 5% annual rate in the quarter.

But over the rest of the year, Federal Reserve officials expect growth to moderate to a long-term rate of 3% to 3.5%, slow enough to keep inflation at bay but fast enough to keep unemployment from rising. That would permit the Fed to stop the campaign of interest-rate increases it began in June 2004.

This week, however, the economy has been sending signals that it might not be ready to settle down. The housing market, though softer, is holding up better than many skeptics predicted and consumer confidence remains surprisingly strong, despite soaring gasoline prices, and concerns about President Bush's handling of the economy. Meanwhile, solid job creation and wage gains are providing a cushion for consumer spending, and orders for capital equipment are picking up.

Yesterday, the Commerce Department said new-home sales jumped 13.8% in March from a month earlier, erasing much of the 15.8% decline seen over the two previous months. It also reported that orders for durable goods -- items such as tractors and washing machines that are meant to last three years or more -- rose 6.1% last month on the back of strong aircraft orders at Boeing Co. More important, orders for capital goods, excluding aircraft and defense, usually a good indicator of future business spending, broke out of a recent slump last month to post a 3% gain.

"What we're seeing now is telling us about the momentum going into the second quarter," said Julia Coronado, senior economist at Barclays Capital. "It seems to be pretty strong."

The flow of indicators these days is particularly important because the Fed has said that prospects for further increases in short-term interest rates -- beyond an expected quarter-point increase next month to 5% from 4.75% -- will depend on what new economic data show. At their last meeting in March, Fed officials appeared to think they could pause after rates reached 5%.

But the spate of strong data has prompted futures markets to raise the probability of further increases to more than 50%, and the inflation-wary bond market has sold off recently. Yesterday, bond prices fell again, and the yield on the benchmark 10-year Treasury note rose to 5.107%.

The Fed's latest roundup of business conditions, also released yesterday, suggested no slowdown is yet under way. "Economic activity continued to expand in March and the first half of April," the Fed said in its "beige book" report. Only one of the 12 Fed districts -- New York -- said growth slowed. Even so, evidence of broader inflationary pressure remained scant. The Cleveland district said many raw-material prices had risen, but "retail prices remained relatively flat."

Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke testifies on the economy to the Joint Economic Committee of Congress today, but he isn't expected to give clear signals about what the Fed's next interest-rate move is likely to be.

To be sure, despite the recent robust data, economists say a strong finish to the first quarter is more likely to be a prelude to slower growth in the second. Oil prices and interest rates are rising, and the extent of the housing market's correction is still uncertain. As a result, few analysts are willing to raise their growth forecasts.

Joshua Shapiro, chief U.S. economist at New York consulting firm MFR Inc., said he still expects growth to slow to a 3% annual rate in the current quarter. "Could the second quarter be a little stronger than that? Yes, but not way higher," he said.

Businesses are helping to spur the economy. On their way to record corporate profits last year, businesses increased their investment at a 16% annual pace in the fourth quarter, the fastest clip since mid-2004. Ms. Coronado of Barclays expects that spending on equipment and software, a closely watched measure of investment, rose at an 11% annual rate in the first quarter and will grow at a 9% rate in the current quarter.

Google Inc. recently said that its capital spending, which was $838 million in 2005, will grow even faster than its revenue this year. Manufacturing bellwether Caterpillar Inc. recently raised its profit forecast for 2006 to between $4.85 and $5.20 a share, up from $4.04 a share in 2005. Announcing the sunnier outlook, Caterpillar Chief Executive James Owens said "strong underlying fundamentals are in place, and we believe this is a business cycle that has staying power."

In yesterday's Fed report, business investment appeared to be strong. "Production capacity for mining equipment in the Chicago district is reportedly booked through 2007," the report said.

With prospects on the business side looking solid, one of the major assumptions built into forecasts for slower growth has been that consumers will cut back on spending. Peter Hooper, chief U.S. economist at Deutsche Bank, said, "We really do need to see some slowing in consumer-spending growth in the second quarter to give the Fed room to pause" its rate raising at 5%. He predicted that would happen thanks to the combined impact of higher gasoline prices and slowing home-price appreciation, "although the risks have risen" that the Fed will raise rates beyond 5%.

The housing market has yet to show signs of a prolonged slump, but even with March's upturn a slowdown is still apparent. New-home sales in March, while up from February, were 11.5% below their peak last July. Sales of existing homes, which make up 80% of the housing market, are only 4.8% lower than when they topped out last June.

However, inventories of new homes on the market rose to 555,000 in March, up 24% from a year ago, and the unadjusted median sales price for a new home actually fell 2% from a year earlier, suggesting builders have used discounts to get homes sold. Existing-home prices have declined slightly so far this year, giving consumers less home equity to finance other purchases.

In another sign of weakness in the housing market, Dallas-based home builder Centex Corp. yesterday said orders fell 11% in the fiscal quarter ended March 31. CEO Timothy Eller said cancellations were up, too. The company slashed its earnings outlook for fiscal 2007 to a range of $8.50 to $10 a share, down from an earlier estimate of $10.75 to $11.25.

Still, the strong labor market, which has lowered the unemployment rate to 4.7% and shows few signs of reversing course, is likely to reassure consumers. Yesterday's Fed report found that job markets were still tightening, and wage pressures were rising. The Dallas district cited "reports of difficultly finding workers who meet basic qualifications of employment, such as background checks and drug tests."

One New York-district employment agency said "salaries are up at least 15% from a year ago for experienced office workers," and a Boston district firm said there was a "war for talent."

Politics &Economics: Confidence Index And Home Sales Display Strength --- Investors Place Their Bets On More Interest-Rate Rises; Real-Estate Soft Landing?
By Christopher Conkey

26 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
(Copyright (c) 2006, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

In two surprising signs of the economy's resilience, sales of existing homes rose slightly in March and consumer confidence strengthened.

The two reports painted positive pictures of the economy at a time when rising gasoline prices threaten to curtail other consumer spending. Investors concluded that the Federal Reserve is now more likely to prolong its campaign of interest-rate increases in an effort to moderate economic growth. "Housing is the most interest-sensitive sector of the economy," wrote Michael Moran, chief economist at Daiwa Securities America Inc. "If this area remains firm, policy makers most likely will conclude that financial conditions are not sufficiently restrictive."

Existing-home sales edged up 0.3% in March after jumping 5% in February, according to the National Association of Realtors. The gains in the past two months followed a gradual decline in the previous five months, and indicate the housing market isn't experiencing the severe downturn some have feared.

Meanwhile, the Conference Board announced that its consumer-confidence index for April rose to its highest level in four years. The 109.6 reading this month was up from 107.5 in March. The survey continues to show consumers are more optimistic about the current economic situation than they are about future conditions. "Recent improvements in the labor market have been a major driver behind the rise in confidence," said Lynn Franco, director of the survey. But she also warned that rising gas prices "could dampen consumers' mood."

Markets anticipate the Fed will raise short-term interest rates next month to 5% from 4.75%, but they are divided over whether it will pause thereafter. In futures-market trading yesterday, the likelihood that the Fed will opt for another quarter-point increase in June rose close to 50% from around 40% on Monday. The inflation-wary bond market moved in the same direction, pushing prices down and driving the yield on the benchmark 10-year Treasury note up to 5.081%, a four-year high.

Despite concerns that the economy isn't cooling enough to satisfy the Fed, there were plenty of reasons for caution in yesterday's housing report. The inventory of unsold homes rose 7% in March to the equivalent of a 5.5-month supply. That's the highest level of homes on the market in nearly eight years, according to the NAR. The supply of condominiums on the market rose 15% to 6.9 months in March, the largest inventory of unsold condos in more than six years.

The median sales price -- half of homes sold for more and half for less -- was unchanged in March at $218,000, down from a peak of $229,000 last June, but 7.4% higher than a year ago. Existing-home sales, which constitute 80% of the housing market, ran at an annual rate of 6.92 million units last month, down 0.7% from a year ago.

Today, the Commerce Department will release data on new-home sales in March.
